<p>There is no agreement on the relationships between and subgrouping of the different Afroasiatic branches.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019251_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019251-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Whereas <a href="/wiki/Marcel_Cohen" title="Marcel Cohen">Marcel Cohen</a> (1947) claimed he saw no evidence for internal subgroupings, numerous other scholars have made proposals,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2018_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2018-66">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> with Carsten Peust counting 27 as of 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeust2012225-227_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeust2012225-227-67">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Common trends in proposals as of 2019 include using common or lacking grammatical features  to argue that Omotic was the first language to branch off, often followed by Chadic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGragg201943_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGragg201943-68">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> In contrast to scholars who argue for an early split of Chadic from Afroasiatic, scholars of the Russian school tend to argue that Chadic and Egyptian are closely related,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlench2006145_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlench2006145-69">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> and scholars who rely on percentage of shared lexicon often group Chadic with Berber.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanker202329_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanker202329-70">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Three scholars who agree on an early split between Omotic and the other subbranches, but little else, are Harold Fleming (1983), Christopher Ehret (1995), and Lionel Bender (1997).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGüldemann2018312–313_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGüldemann2018312–313-71">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> In contrast, scholars relying on shared lexicon often produce a Cushitic-Omotic group.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanker202329_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanker202329-70">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, the minority of scholars who favor an Asian origin of Afroasiatic tend to place Semitic as the first branch to split off.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlench2006144_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlench2006144-72">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Disagreement on which features are innovative and which are inherited from Proto-Afroasiatic produces radically different trees, as can be seen by comparing the trees produced by Ehret and <a href="/wiki/Igor_Diakonoff" class="mw-redirect" title="Igor Diakonoff">Igor Diakonoff</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBacovcinWilson2018422_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBacovcinWilson2018422-73">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Responding to the above, <a href="/wiki/Tom_G%C3%BCldemann" title="Tom Güldemann">Tom Güldemann</a> criticizes attempts at finding subgroupings based on common or lacking morphology by arguing that the presence or absence of morphological features is not a useful way of discerning subgroupings in Afroasiatic, because it cannot be excluded that families currently lacking certain features had them in the past; this also means that the presence of morphological features cannot be taken as defining a subgroup.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGüldemann2018310_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGüldemann2018310-74">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Peust notes that other factors that can obscure genetic relationships between languages include the poor state of present documentation and understanding of particular language families (historically with Egyptian, presently with Omotic).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeust2012227_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeust2012227-75">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Gene Gragg likewise argues that more needs to be known about Omotic still, and that Afroasiatic linguists have still not found convincing <a href="/wiki/Isogloss" title="Isogloss">isoglosses</a> on which to base genetic distinctions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGragg201943_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGragg201943-68">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>One way of avoiding the problem of determining which features are original and which are inherited is to use a computational methodology such as <a href="/wiki/Lexicostatistics" title="Lexicostatistics">lexicostatistics</a>, with one of the earliest attempts being Fleming 1983.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlench2006144_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlench2006144-72">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> This is also the method used by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Militarev" title="Alexander Militarev">Alexander Militarev</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sergei_Starostin" title="Sergei Starostin">Sergei Starostin</a> to create a family tree.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMilitarev2005398–399_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMilitarev2005398–399-76">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlažek20131_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlažek20131-77">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Fleming (2006) was a more recent attempt by Fleming, with a different result from Militarev and Starostin.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeust2012227_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeust2012227-75">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Hezekiah Bacovcin and David Wilson argue that this methodology is invalid for discerning linguistic sub-relationship.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBacovcinWilson2018422_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBacovcinWilson2018422-73">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> They note the method's inability to detect various strong commonalities even between well-studied branches of AA.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBacovcinWilson2018427_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBacovcinWilson2018427-78">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification_history">Classification history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afroasiatic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Classification history"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A relationship between Hebrew, Arabic, and Aramaic and the Berber languages was perceived as early as the 9th century CE by the Hebrew grammarian and physician <a href="/wiki/Judah_ibn_Kuraish" title="Judah ibn Kuraish">Judah ibn Quraysh</a>, who is regarded as a forerunner of Afroasiatic studies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELipiński200121_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELipiński200121-79">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> The French orientalist <a href="/wiki/Guillaume_Postel" title="Guillaume Postel">Guillaume Postel</a> had also pointed out similarities between Hebrew, Arabic, and Aramaic in 1538, and <a href="/wiki/Hiob_Ludolf" title="Hiob Ludolf">Hiob Ludolf</a> noted similarities also to Ge'ez and Amharic in 1701. This family was formally described and named "Semitic" by <a href="/wiki/August_Ludwig_von_Schl%C3%B6zer" title="August Ludwig von Schlözer">August Ludwig von Schlözer</a> in 1781.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngierShay20124_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngierShay20124-80">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> In 1844, <a href="/wiki/Theodor_Benfey" title="Theodor Benfey">Theodor Benfey</a> first described the relationship between Semitic and the Egyptian language and connected both to the Berber and the Cushitic languages (which he called "Ethiopic").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELipiński200121_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELipiński200121-79">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> In the same year T.N. Newman suggested a relationship between Semitic and the Hausa language, an idea that was taken up by early scholars of Afroasiatic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPorkhomovsky2020270_12-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPorkhomovsky2020270-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> In 1855, <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Renan" class="mw-redirect" title="Ernst Renan">Ernst Renan</a> named these languages, related to Semitic but not Semitic, "Hamitic," in 1860 Carl Lottner proposed that they belonged to a single language family, and in 1876 <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_M%C3%BCller_(linguist)" title="Friedrich Müller (linguist)">Friedrich Müller</a> first described them as a "Hamito-Semitic" language family.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPorkhomovsky2020269_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPorkhomovsky2020269-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Müller assumed that there existed a distinct "Hamitic" branch of the family that consisted of Egyptian, Berber, and Cushitic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngierShay20124_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngierShay20124-80">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> He did not include the Chadic languages, though contemporary Egyptologist <a href="/wiki/Karl_Richard_Lepsius" title="Karl Richard Lepsius">Karl Richard Lepsius</a> argued for the relation of Hausa to the Berber languages.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHayward200084_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHayward200084-81">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Some scholars would continue to regard Hausa as related to the other Afroasiatic languages, but the idea was controversial: many scholars refused to admit that the largely unwritten, "<a href="/wiki/Negroid" title="Negroid">Negroid</a>" Chadic languages were in the same family as the "<a href="/wiki/Caucasian_race" title="Caucasian race">Caucasian</a>" ancient civilizations of the Egyptians and Semites.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERuhlen199187–88_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERuhlen199187–88-82">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPorkhomovsky2020271_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPorkhomovsky2020271-83">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Africa_ethnic_groups_1996.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Africa_ethnic_groups_1996.png/290px-Africa_ethnic_groups_1996.png" decoding="async" width="290" height="346" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Africa_ethnic_groups_1996.png/435px-Africa_ethnic_groups_1996.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Africa_ethnic_groups_1996.png/580px-Africa_ethnic_groups_1996.png 2x" data-file-width="8651" data-file-height="10315" /></a><figcaption>Distribution of ethnic groups in Africa (Afroasiatic/Hamito-Semitic-speaking in yellow)</figcaption></figure>
<p>An important development in the history of Afroasiatic scholarship – and the history of African linguistics – was the creation of the "<a href="/wiki/Hamites" title="Hamites">Hamitic theory</a>" or "Hamitic hypothesis" by Lepsius, fellow Egyptologist <a href="/wiki/Christian_Bunsen" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian Bunsen">Christian Bunsen</a>, and linguist <a href="/w/index.php?title=Christian_Bleek&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Christian Bleek (page does not exist)">Christian Bleek</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolleveld2020204-205_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolleveld2020204-205-84">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> This theory connected the "Hamites", the originators of Hamitic languages, with (supposedly culturally superior) "Caucasians", who were assumed to have migrated into Africa and intermixed with indigenous "Negroid" Africans in ancient times.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019251_58-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019251-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> The "Hamitic theory" would serve as the basis for <a href="/wiki/Carl_Meinhof" title="Carl Meinhof">Carl Meinhof</a>'s highly influential classification of African languages in his 1912 book <i><span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Die Sprache der Hamiten</i></span></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGüldemann201861_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGüldemann201861-85">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> On one hand, the "Hamitic" classification was justified partially based on linguistic features: for example, Meinhof split the presently-understood Chadic family into "Hamito-Chadic", and an unrelated non-Hamitic "Chadic" based on which languages possessed grammatical gender.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPorkhomovsky2020271_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPorkhomovsky2020271-83">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> On the other hand, the classification also relied on non-linguistic anthropological and culturally contingent features, such as skin color, hair type, and lifestyle.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERuhlen199182–83_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERuhlen199182–83-86">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Ultimately, Meinhof's classification of Hamitic proved to include languages from every presently-recognized language family within Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHayward200084_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHayward200084-81">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The first scholar to question the existence of "Hamitic languages" was <a href="/wiki/Marcel_Cohen" title="Marcel Cohen">Marcel Cohen</a> in  1924,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngierShay20125_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngierShay20125-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> with skepticism also expressed by A. Klingenheben and Dietrich Westermann during the 1920s and '30s.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019251_58-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019251-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> However, Meinhof's "Hamitic" classification remained prevalent throughout the early 20th century until it was definitively disproven by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Greenberg" title="Joseph Greenberg">Joseph Greenberg</a> in the 1940s, based on racial and anthropological data.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPorkhomovsky2020271_83-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPorkhomovsky2020271-83">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGüldemann2018309_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGüldemann2018309-87">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Instead, Greenberg proposed an Afroasiatic family consisting of five branches: Berber, Chadic, Cushitic, Egyptian, and Semitic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019251_58-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019251-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGragg201922_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGragg201922-88">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngierShay20125_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngierShay20125-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Reluctance among some scholars to recognize Chadic as a branch of Afroasiatic persisted as late as the 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPorkhomovsky2020271_83-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPorkhomovsky2020271-83">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> In 1969, <a href="/wiki/Harold_C._Fleming" title="Harold C. Fleming">Harold Fleming</a> proposed that a group of languages classified by Greenberg as Cushitic were in fact their own independent "Omotic" branch—a proposal that has been widely, if not universally, accepted.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019251_58-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019251-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> These six branches now constitute an academic consensus on the genetic structure of the family.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPorkhomovsky2020272_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPorkhomovsky2020272-89">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Greenberg relied on his own method of <a href="/wiki/Mass_comparison" title="Mass comparison">mass comparison</a> of vocabulary items rather than the <a href="/wiki/Comparative_method" title="Comparative method">comparative method</a> of demonstrating regular sound correspondences to establish the family.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHayward200086–87_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHayward200086–87-90">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> An alternative classification, based on the pronominal and conjugation systems, was proposed by A.N. Tucker in 1967.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHodge197111_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHodge197111-91">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> As of 2023, widely accepted sound correspondences between the different branches have not yet been firmly established.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinand202340_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinand202340-92">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuehnergard2023140_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuehnergard2023140-93">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, morphological traits attributable to the proto-language and the establishment of cognates throughout the family have confirmed its <a href="/wiki/Genetic_relationship_(linguistics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Genetic relationship (linguistics)">genetic validity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGüldemann2018347_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGüldemann2018347-94">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngierShay20123_9-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngierShay20123-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin">Origin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afroasiatic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Origin"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Afroasiatic_homeland" class="mw-redirect" title="Afroasiatic homeland">Afroasiatic homeland</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Timeline">Timeline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afroasiatic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Timeline"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There is no consensus as to when Proto-Afroasiatic was spoken.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019252_43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019252-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The absolute latest date for when Proto-Afroasiatic could have been extant is <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;4000 BCE</span>, after which Egyptian and the Semitic languages are firmly attested. However, in all likelihood these languages began to diverge well before this hard boundary.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGragg201943_68-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGragg201943-68">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> The estimations offered by scholars as to when Proto-Afroasiatic was spoken vary widely, ranging from 18,000<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>BCE to 8,000<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019252_43-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019252-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> An estimate at the youngest end of this range still makes Afroasiatic the oldest proven language family.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENichols2003300_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENichols2003300-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Contrasting proposals of an early emergence, Tom Güldemann has argued that less time may have been required for the divergence than is usually assumed, as it is possible for a language to rapidly restructure due to <a href="/wiki/Language_contact" title="Language contact">areal contact</a>, with the evolution of Chadic (and likely also Omotic) serving as pertinent examples.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGüldemann2018311_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGüldemann2018311-95">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Location">Location</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afroasiatic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Location"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Likewise, no consensus exists as to where proto-Afroasiatic originated.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019252_43-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019252-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Scholars have proposed locations for the Afroasiatic homeland across Africa and West Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngierShay201213_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngierShay201213-96">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Roger Blench writes that the debate possesses "a strong ideological flavor", with associations between an Asian origin and "high civilization".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlench2006144_72-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlench2006144-72">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> An additional complicating factor is the lack of agreement on the subgroupings of Afroasiatic (see <a href="#Further_subdivisions">Further subdivisions</a>) – this makes associating archaeological evidence with the spread of Afroasiatic particularly difficult.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlench2006148–150_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlench2006148–150-97">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, there is a long-accepted link between the speakers of Proto-<a href="/wiki/Southern_Cushitic_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Southern Cushitic languages">Southern Cushitic languages</a> and the East African <a href="/wiki/Savanna_Pastoral_Neolithic" title="Savanna Pastoral Neolithic">Savanna Pastoral Neolithic</a> (5000 years ago), and archaeological evidence associates the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Cushitic" class="mw-redirect" title="Proto-Cushitic">Proto-Cushitic</a> speakers with economic transformations in the Sahara dating c. 8,500 ago, as well as the speakers of the <a href="/wiki/Zenati_languages" title="Zenati languages">Proto-Zenati</a> variety of the Berber languages with an expansion across the <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a> in the 5th century CE.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEhret_et_al.2023270_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEhret_et_al.2023270-98">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>An origin somewhere on the African continent has broad scholarly support,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGragg201943_68-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGragg201943-68">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> and is seen as being well-supported by the linguistic data.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier201213_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier201213-99">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Most scholars more narrowly place the homeland near the geographic center of its present distribution,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuehnergard2004138_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuehnergard2004138-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> "in the southeastern Sahara or adjacent Horn of Africa."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEhretKeitaNewman20041680_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEhretKeitaNewman20041680-100">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> The Afroasiatic languages spoken in Africa are not more closely related to each other than they are to Semitic, as one would expect if only Semitic had remained in an West Asian homeland while all other branches had spread from there.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStarostin2017226_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStarostin2017226-101">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Likewise, all Semitic languages are fairly similar to each other, whereas the African branches of Afroasiatic are very diverse; this suggests the rapid spread of Semitic out of Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGragg201943_68-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGragg201943-68">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Proponents of an origin of Afroasiatic within Africa assume the proto-language to have been spoken by pre-Neolithic <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherers" class="mw-redirect" title="Hunter-gatherers">hunter-gatherers</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGüldemann2018311_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGüldemann2018311-95">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> arguing that there is no evidence of words in Proto-Afroasiatic related to agriculture or animal husbandry.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEhretKeitaNewman20041680_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEhretKeitaNewman20041680-100">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Christopher Ehret, S.O. Y. Keita, and Paul Newman also argue that archaeology does not indicate a spread of migrating farmers into Africa, but rather a gradual incorporation of animal husbandry into indigenous foraging cultures.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEhretKeitaNewman20041680–1681_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEhretKeitaNewman20041680–1681-102">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Ehret, in a separate publication, argued that the two principles in linguistic approaches for determining the origin of languages which are the principles of fewest moves and greatest diversity had put “beyond reasonable doubt” that the language family “had originated in the Horn of Africa”.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A significant minority of scholars supports an Asian origin of Afroasiatic,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlench2006144_72-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlench2006144-72">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> most of whom are specialists in Semitic or Egyptian studies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlench2006150_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlench2006150-104">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> The main proponent of an Asian origin is the linguist <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Militarev" title="Alexander Militarev">Alexander Militarev</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlmansa-VillatoroŠtubňová_Nigrelli20235_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlmansa-VillatoroŠtubňová_Nigrelli20235-105">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> who argues that Proto-Afroasiatic was spoken by early agriculturalists in the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> and subsequently spread to Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019252_43-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019252-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Militarev associates the speakers of Proto-Afroasiatic with the Levantine Post-<a href="/wiki/Natufian_Culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Natufian Culture">Natufian Culture</a>, arguing that the reconstructed lexicon of flora and fauna, as well as farming and pastoralist vocabulary indicates that Proto-AA must have been spoken in this area.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMilitarev2002_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMilitarev2002-106">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMilitarev2009_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMilitarev2009-107">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> Scholar <a href="/wiki/Jared_Diamond" title="Jared Diamond">Jared Diamond</a> and archaeologist <a href="/wiki/Peter_Bellwood" title="Peter Bellwood">Peter Bellwood</a> have taken up Militarev's arguments as part of their general argument that the spread of linguistic <a href="/wiki/Macrofamily" title="Macrofamily">macrofamilies</a> (such as Indo-European, Bantu, and Austro-Asiatic) can be associated with the development of agriculture; they argue that there is clear archaeological support for farming spreading from the Levant into Africa via the Nile valley.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDiamondBellwood2003601_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDiamondBellwood2003601-108">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonological_characteristics">Phonological characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afroasiatic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Phonological characteristics"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><span><audio id="mwe_player_0" controls="" preload="none" class="mw-file-element" width="180" style="width:180px;" data-durationhint="71" data-mwtitle="Tamazightchelha.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" resource="/wiki/File:Tamazightchelha.ogg"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fe/Tamazightchelha.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-width="0" data-height="0" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/f/fe/Tamazightchelha.ogg/Tamazightchelha.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" /></audio></span><figcaption>Speech sample in <a href="/wiki/Shilha_language" title="Shilha language">Shilha</a> (<a href="/wiki/Berber_languages" title="Berber languages">Berber</a> branch)</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><span><audio id="mwe_player_1" controls="" preload="none" class="mw-file-element" width="180" style="width:180px;" data-durationhint="61" data-mwtitle="Assyriansample.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" resource="/wiki/File:Assyriansample.ogg"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1d/Assyriansample.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-width="0" data-height="0" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/1/1d/Assyriansample.ogg/Assyriansample.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" /></audio></span><figcaption>Speech sample in the Semitic <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Neo-Aramaic" class="mw-redirect" title="Assyrian Neo-Aramaic">Neo-Aramaic</a> language, a descendant of <a href="/wiki/Old_Aramaic" title="Old Aramaic">Old Aramaic</a></figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><span><audio id="mwe_player_2" controls="" preload="none" class="mw-file-element" width="180" style="width:180px;" data-durationhint="62" data-mwtitle="Shaxmednuradc1.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" resource="/wiki/File:Shaxmednuradc1.ogg"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c9/Shaxmednuradc1.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-width="0" data-height="0" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/c/c9/Shaxmednuradc1.ogg/Shaxmednuradc1.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" /></audio></span><figcaption>Speech sample in <a href="/wiki/Somali_language" title="Somali language">Somali</a> (<a href="/wiki/Cushitic_languages" title="Cushitic languages">Cushitic</a> branch)</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><span><audio id="mwe_player_3" controls="" preload="none" class="mw-file-element" width="180" style="width:180px;" data-durationhint="44" data-mwtitle="Poem_by_Abu_&#39;ala_al-Ma&#39;arri_(&quot;I_no_longer_steal_from_nature&quot;)_read_in_Arabic.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" resource="/wiki/File:Poem_by_Abu_%27ala_al-Ma%27arri_(%22I_no_longer_steal_from_nature%22)_read_in_Arabic.ogg"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/28/Poem_by_Abu_%27ala_al-Ma%27arri_%28%22I_no_longer_steal_from_nature%22%29_read_in_Arabic.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-width="0" data-height="0" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/2/28/Poem_by_Abu_%27ala_al-Ma%27arri_%28%22I_no_longer_steal_from_nature%22%29_read_in_Arabic.ogg/Poem_by_Abu_%27ala_al-Ma%27arri_%28%22I_no_longer_steal_from_nature%22%29_read_in_Arabic.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" /></audio></span><figcaption>Speech sample in <a href="/wiki/Classical_Arabic" title="Classical Arabic">Classical Arabic</a> (<a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">Semitic</a> branch)</figcaption></figure>
<p>Afroasiatic languages share a number of phonetic and phonological features.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019263_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019263-109">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Syllable_structure">Syllable structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afroasiatic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Syllable structure"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Egyptian, Cushitic, Berber, Omotic, and most languages in the Semitic branch all require a syllable to begin with a consonant (with the exception of some grammatical prefixes).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012511_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012511-110">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Igor_Diakonoff" class="mw-redirect" title="Igor Diakonoff">Igor Diakonoff</a> argues that this constraint goes back to Proto-Afroasiatic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDiakonoff198842_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDiakonoff198842-111">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Some Chadic languages allow a syllable to begin with a vowel;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012511_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012511-110">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> however, in many Chadic languages verbs must begin with a consonant. In Cushitic and Chadic languages, a glottal stop or glottal fricative may be inserted to prevent a word from beginning with a vowel.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2018_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2018-66">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Typically, syllables only begin with a single consonant.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019304_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019304-112">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With the exception of some Chadic languages, all Afroasiatic languages allow both closed and open syllables; many Chadic languages do not allow a syllable to end in a consonant.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012511-512_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012511-512-113">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Most words end in a vowel in Omotic and Cushitic, making syllable-final consonant clusters rare.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019304_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019304-112">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Diakonoff argues that proto-Afroasiatic syllables disallowed consonant clusters or vowels at the end of a syllable.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDiakonoff198842_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDiakonoff198842-111">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Syllable_weight" title="Syllable weight">Syllable weight</a> plays an important role in AA, especially in Chadic; it can affect the form of affixes attached to a word.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012512_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012512-114">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consonant_systems">Consonant systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afroasiatic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Consonant systems"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Several Afroasiatic languages have large consonant inventories, and it is likely that this is inherited from proto-Afroasiatic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHayward200094_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHayward200094-115">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> All Afroasiatic languages contain <a href="/wiki/Stop_consonant" class="mw-redirect" title="Stop consonant">stops</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fricative" title="Fricative">fricatives</a>; some branches have additional types of consonants such as <a href="/wiki/Affricate" title="Affricate">affricates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lateral_consonant" title="Lateral consonant">lateral consonants</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012508_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012508-116">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> AA languages tend to have <a href="/wiki/Pharyngeal_consonant" title="Pharyngeal consonant">pharyngeal</a> <a href="/wiki/Fricative" title="Fricative">fricative</a> consonants, with Egyptian, Semitic, Berber, and Cushitic sharing <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_pharyngeal_fricative" title="Voiceless pharyngeal fricative">ħ</a>/</span> and <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiced_pharyngeal_fricative" title="Voiced pharyngeal fricative">ʕ</a>/</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019264_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019264-117">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> In all AA languages, consonants can be <a href="/wiki/Bilabial" class="mw-redirect" title="Bilabial">bilabial</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alveolar" class="mw-redirect" title="Alveolar">alveolar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Velar" class="mw-redirect" title="Velar">velar</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Glottal_consonant" title="Glottal consonant">glottal</a>, with additional places of articulation found in some branches or languages.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012508_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012508-116">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, the <a href="/wiki/Glottal_stop" title="Glottal stop">glottal stop</a> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Glottal_stop" title="Glottal stop">ʔ</a>/</span> usually exists as a phoneme, and there tends to be no phonemic contrast between <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_bilabial_plosive" title="Voiceless bilabial plosive">p</a>&#93;</span> and <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_labiodental_fricative" title="Voiceless labiodental fricative">f</a>&#93;</span> or <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Voiced_bilabial_plosive" title="Voiced bilabial plosive">b</a>&#93;</span> and <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Voiced_labiodental_fricative" title="Voiced labiodental fricative">v</a>&#93;</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019264_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019264-117">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> In Cushitic, the Ethiopian Semitic language <a href="/wiki/Tigrinya" class="mw-redirect" title="Tigrinya">Tigrinya</a>, and some Chadic languages, there is no underlying phoneme <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_bilabial_plosive" title="Voiceless bilabial plosive">p</a>/</span> at all.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012509_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012509-118">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Most, if not all, branches of Afroasiatic distinguish between <a href="/wiki/Voiceless" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiceless">voiceless</a>, <a href="/wiki/Voiced" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiced">voiced</a>, and "<a href="/wiki/Emphatic_consonant" title="Emphatic consonant">emphatic</a>" consonants.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHetzron2009548_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHetzron2009548-119">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;d&#93;</a></sup> The emphatic consonants are typically formed deeper in the throat than the others;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019264_117-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019264-117">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> they can be realized variously as <a href="/wiki/Glottalic_consonant" title="Glottalic consonant">glottalized</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pharyngealization" title="Pharyngealization">pharyngealized</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uvularization" title="Uvularization">uvularized</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ejective" class="mw-redirect" title="Ejective">ejective</a>, and/or <a href="/wiki/Implosive" class="mw-redirect" title="Implosive">implosive</a> consonants in the different branches.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHetzron2009548_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHetzron2009548-119">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> It is generally agreed that only the <a href="/wiki/Obstruent" title="Obstruent">obstruents</a> had a contrast between voiceless and voiced forms in Proto-Afroasiatic, whereas <a href="/wiki/Continuant" title="Continuant">continuants</a> were voiceless.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngierShay201210_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngierShay201210-122">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A form of long-distance consonant <a href="/wiki/Assimilation_(linguistics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Assimilation (linguistics)">assimilation</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Consonant_harmony" title="Consonant harmony">consonant harmony</a> is attested in Berber, Chadic, Cushitic, and Semitic: it usually affects features such as pharyngealization, <a href="/wiki/Palatalization_(phonetics)" title="Palatalization (phonetics)">palatalization</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Labialization" title="Labialization">labialization</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012517_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012517-123">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> Several Omotic languages have "<a href="/wiki/Sibilant" title="Sibilant">sibilant</a> harmony", meaning that all sibilants (s, sh, z, ts, etc.) in a word must match.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAmha2012439–440_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmha2012439–440-124">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consonant_incompatibility">Consonant incompatibility</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afroasiatic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Consonant incompatibility"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="wikitable" style="float:right; font-size:smaller;">
<caption>Examples of root consonant incompatibilities from Egyptian, after <a href="#CITEREFAllen2020a">Allen 2020a</a>
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th>consonant</th>
<th>cannot occur with
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>p</td>
<td>b, f, m, h
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>r</td>
<td>ꜣ, b
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>ḫ</td>
<td>h, ḥ, ẖ, q, k, g, ṯ, ḏ
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>s</td>
<td>ḥ, z
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>t</td>
<td>ꜥ, z, q, g, d, ḏ
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Restrictions against the co-occurrence of certain, usually similar, consonants in verbal roots can be found in all Afroasiatic branches, though they are only weakly attested in Chadic and Omotic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBender19789-10_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBender19789-10-125">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> The most widespread constraint is against two different <a href="/wiki/Labial_consonant" title="Labial consonant">labial consonants</a> (other than <i>w</i>) occurring together in a root, a constraint which can be found in all branches but Omotic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEhret199577,_488_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEhret199577,_488-126">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Another widespread constraint is against two non-identical <a href="/wiki/Lateral_consonant" title="Lateral consonant">lateral</a> <a href="/wiki/Obstruent" title="Obstruent">obstruents</a>, which can be found in Egyptian, Chadic, Semitic, and probably Cushitic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEhret1995395_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEhret1995395-127">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> Such rules do not always apply for nouns, numerals, or <a href="/wiki/Denominal_verb" title="Denominal verb">denominal verbs</a>, and do not affect prefixes or suffixes added to the root.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenberg1950a178_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenberg1950a178-128">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Roots that may have contained sequences that were possible in Proto-Afroasiatic but are disallowed in the daughter languages are assumed to have undergone consonant <a href="/wiki/Dissimilation" title="Dissimilation">dissimilation</a> or <a href="/wiki/Assimilation_(linguistics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Assimilation (linguistics)">assimilation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdzard1992153-154_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdzard1992153-154-129">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETakács1999323–332_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETakács1999323–332-130">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A set of constraints, developed originally by Joseph Greenberg on the basis of Arabic, has been claimed to be typical for Afroasiatic languages.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVernet20111_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVernet20111-131">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> Greenberg divided Semitic consonants into four types: "back consonants" (<a href="/wiki/Glottal_consonant" title="Glottal consonant">glottal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pharyngeal_consonant" title="Pharyngeal consonant">pharyngeal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uvular_consonant" title="Uvular consonant">uvular</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laryngeal_consonant" title="Laryngeal consonant">laryngeal</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Velar_consonant" title="Velar consonant">velar consonants</a>), "front consonants" (<a href="/wiki/Dental_consonant" title="Dental consonant">dental</a> or <a href="/wiki/Alveolar_consonant" title="Alveolar consonant">alveolar consonants</a>), <a href="/wiki/Liquid_consonant" title="Liquid consonant">liquid consonants</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Labial_consonant" title="Labial consonant">labial consonants</a>. He showed that, generally, any consonant from one of these groups could combine with consonants from any other group, but could not be used together with consonants from the same group.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenberg1950a178_128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenberg1950a178-128">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, he showed that Proto-Semitic restricted a sequence of two identical consonants in the first and second position of the triliteral root.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenberg1950a167–168_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenberg1950a167–168-132">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> These rules also have a number of exceptions:
</p>
<ol><li>velar consonants can occur with pharyngeals or laryngeals;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVernet20117_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVernet20117-133">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>dental consonants can co-occur with <a href="/wiki/Sibilant" title="Sibilant">sibilants</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBender197810_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBender197810-134">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> However, there are no Proto-Semitic verbal roots with <i>ḍ</i> and a sibilant, and roots with <i>d</i> and a sibilant are uncommon. In all attested cases of a dental and a sibilant, the sibilant occurs in first position and the dental in second.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVernet20117_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVernet20117-133">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup></li></ol>
<p>Similar exceptions can be demonstrated for the other AA branches that have these restrictions to their root formation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBender197810_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBender197810-134">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/James_P._Allen" class="mw-redirect" title="James P. Allen">James P. Allen</a> has demonstrated that slightly different rules apply to Egyptian: for instance, Egyptian allows two identical consonants in some roots, and disallows velars from occurring with pharyngeals.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen2020a90-92_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen2020a90-92-135">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vowel_systems">Vowel systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afroasiatic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Vowel systems"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There is a large variety of vocalic systems in AA,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019264_117-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019264-117">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> and attempts to reconstruct the vocalic system of Proto-Afroasiatic vary considerably.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngierShay201210_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngierShay201210-122">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>
All branches of Afroasiatic have a limited number of underlying vowels (between two and seven), but the number of phonetic vowels can be much larger. The quality of the underlying vowels varies considerably by language; the most common vowel throughout AA is <a href="/wiki/Schwa" title="Schwa">schwa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012621_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012621-136">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> In the different languages, central vowels are often inserted to break up consonant clusters (a form of <a href="/wiki/Epenthesis" title="Epenthesis">epenthesis</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019264_117-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019264-117">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>  Various Semitic, Cushitic, Berber, and Chadic languages, including Arabic, Amharic, Berber, Somali, and East Dangla, also exhibit various types of <a href="/wiki/Vowel_harmony" title="Vowel harmony">vowel harmony</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012517–519_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012517–519-137">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tones">Tones</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afroasiatic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Tones"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The majority of AA languages are <a href="/wiki/Tonal_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Tonal language">tonal languages</a>: phonemic tonality is found in Omotic, Chadic, and Cushitic languages, but absent in Berber and Semitic. There is no information on whether Egyptian had tones.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019265_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019265-138">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> In contemporary Omotic, Chadic, and Cushitic languages, tone is primarily a grammatical feature: it encodes various grammatical functions, only differentiating lexical roots in a few cases.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier201212_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier201212-139">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> In some Chadic and some Omotic languages every syllable has to have a tone, whereas in most Cushitic languages this is not the case.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012513_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012513-140">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> Some scholars postulate that Proto-Afroasiatic may have had tone, while others believe it arose later from a <a href="/wiki/Pitch_accent" class="mw-redirect" title="Pitch accent">pitch accent</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019265_138-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019265-138">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable">

<h2><span id="Similarities_in_grammar.2C_syntax.2C_and_morphology"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Similarities_in_grammar,_syntax,_and_morphology">Similarities in grammar, syntax, and morphology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afroasiatic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Similarities in grammar, syntax, and morphology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>At present, there is no generally accepted reconstruction of Proto-Afroasiatic grammar, syntax, or morphology, nor one for any of the sub-branches besides Egyptian. This means that it is difficult to know which features in Afroasiatic languages are retentions, and which are innovations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngierShay2020572-573_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngierShay2020572-573-141">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> Moreover, all Afroasiatic languages have long been in contact with other language families and with each other, leading to the possibility of widespread borrowing both within Afroasiatic and from unrelated languages.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShay2014574_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShay2014574-142">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> There are nevertheless a number of commonly observed features in Afroasiatic morphology and derivation, including, the use of <a href="/wiki/Suffix" title="Suffix">suffixes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Infix" title="Infix">infixes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vowel_length" title="Vowel length">vowel lengthening</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vowel_reduction" title="Vowel reduction">shortening</a> as a morphological change, as well as the use of tone changes to indicate morphology.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012529–530_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012529–530-143">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> Further commonalities and differences are explored in more detail below.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="General_features">General features</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afroasiatic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: General features"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Consonantal_root_structures">Consonantal root structures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afroasiatic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Consonantal root structures"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A widely attested feature in AA languages is a consonantal structure into which various vocalic "templates" are placed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019265_138-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019265-138">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> This structure is particularly visible in the verbs,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGragg201936_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGragg201936-144">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> and is particularly noticeable in Semitic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019265_138-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019265-138">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> Besides for Semitic, vocalic templates are well attested for Cushitic and Berber,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGragg201937_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGragg201937-145">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> where, along with Chadic, it is less productive; it is absent in Omotic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019265_138-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019265-138">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> For Egyptian, evidence for the root-and-template structure exists from Coptic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGragg201937_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGragg201937-145">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> In Semitic, Egyptian, Berber, verbs have no inherent vowels at all; the vowels found in a given stem are dependent on the vocalic template.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012622_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012622-146">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> In Chadic, verb stems can include an inherent vowel as well.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2018_66-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2018-66">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Most Semitic verbs are <a href="/wiki/Semitic_root" title="Semitic root">triliteral</a> (have three consonants), whereas most Chadic, Omotic, and Cushitic verbs are biliteral (having two consonants).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHayward200093_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHayward200093-147">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> The degree to which the Proto-AA verbal root was triliteral is debated.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGüldemann2018311_95-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGüldemann2018311-95">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> It may have originally been mostly biconsonantal, to which various affixes (such as <a href="#Verbal_extensions">verbal extensions</a>) were then added and lexicalized.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019265_138-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019265-138">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> Although any root could theoretically be used to create a noun or a verb, there is evidence for the existence of distinct noun and verb roots, which behave in different ways.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShay2014576_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShay2014576-148">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="font-size:smaller;">

<p>As part of these templates, the alternation (<a href="/wiki/Apophony" title="Apophony">apophony</a>) between high vowels (e.g. i, u) and a low vowel (a) in verbal forms is usually described as one of the main characteristics of AA languages: this change codes a variety of different functions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012593_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012593-149">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> It is unclear whether this system is a common AA trait;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGragg201938_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGragg201938-150">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> the Chadic examples, for instance, show signs of originally deriving from affixes, which could explain the origins of the alterations in other languages as well.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012593_149-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012593-149">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Word_order">Word order</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afroasiatic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Word order"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>It remains unclear what <a href="/wiki/Word_order" title="Word order">word order</a> Proto-Afroasiatic had.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngierShay201213_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngierShay201213-96">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019269_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019269-151">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> Berber, Egyptian, and most Semitic languages are <a href="/wiki/Verb%E2%80%93subject%E2%80%93object_word_order" title="Verb–subject–object word order">verb-initial languages</a>, whereas Cushitic, Omotic and some Semitic subgroups are <a href="/wiki/Subject%E2%80%93object%E2%80%93verb_word_order" title="Subject–object–verb word order">verb-final languages</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGüldemann2018319_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGüldemann2018319-152">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> Proto-Chadic is reconstructed as having verb-initial word order,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier201213_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier201213-99">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> but most Chadic languages have <a href="/wiki/Subject-verb-object_word_order" class="mw-redirect" title="Subject-verb-object word order">subject-verb-object word order</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019269_151-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019269-151">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Reduplication_and_gemination">Reduplication and gemination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afroasiatic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Reduplication and gemination"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Afroasiatic Languages use the processes of <a href="/wiki/Reduplication" title="Reduplication">reduplication</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gemination" title="Gemination">gemination</a> (which often overlap in meaning) to derive nouns, verbs, adjectives, and adverbs throughout the AA language family. Gemination in particular is one of the typical features of AA.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012531_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012531-153">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> Full or partial reduplication of the verb is often used to derive forms showing repeated action (<a href="/wiki/Pluractionality" title="Pluractionality">pluractionality</a>), though it is unclear if this is an inherited feature or has been widely borrowed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019268_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019268-154">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nouns">Nouns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afroasiatic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Nouns"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Grammatical_gender_and_number">Grammatical gender and number</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afroasiatic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Grammatical gender and number"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<table class="wikitable" style="font-size:smaller;">
<caption>Use of T on feminine nouns, using data from <a href="#CITEREFSouag2023">Souag 2023</a>
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th>Kabyle (Berber)</th>
<th>Hausa (Chadic)</th>
<th>Beja (Cushitic)</th>
<th>Egyptian</th>
<th>Arabic (Semitic)
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>wəl-t 'daughter'</td>
<td>yārinyà-r̃ 'the girl' (r̃ &lt; final -t)<br /></td>
<td>ʔo:(r)-t 'a daughter'<br /> t-ʔo:r 'the daughter'</td>
<td>zꜣ-t 'daughter'</td>
<td>bin-t 'daughter'
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The assignment of nouns and pronouns to either masculine or feminine gender is present in all branches – but not all languages – of the Afroasiatic family.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012522_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012522-155">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> This sex-based gender system is widely agreed to derive from Proto-Afroasiatic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGüldemann2018319_152-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGüldemann2018319-152">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> In most branches, gender is an inherent property of nouns.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012523_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012523-156">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, even when nouns are not cognates, they tend to have the same gender throughout Afroasiatic ("gender stability").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019266_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019266-157">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> In Egyptian, Semitic, and Berber, a feminine suffix -t is attested to mark feminine nouns; in some Cushitic and Chadic languages, a feminine -t suffix or prefix (lexicalized from a demonstrative) is used to mark definiteness.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESouag2023308_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESouag2023308-158">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> In addition to these uses, -t also functions as a <a href="/wiki/Diminutive" title="Diminutive">diminutive</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pejorative" title="Pejorative">pejorative</a>, and/or <a href="/wiki/Singulative" class="mw-redirect" title="Singulative">singulative</a> marker in some languages.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012522_155-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012522-155">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="font-size:smaller;">

<td>aràn

<p>Afroasiatic languages have a variety of ways of marking plurals; in some branches, nouns change gender from singular to plural (gender polarity),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019266_157-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019266-157">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> while in others, plural forms are ungendered.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012253_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012253-159">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> In addition to marking plurals via a number of affixes (with the suffixes -*uu/-*w and -*n(a) widely attested), several AA languages make use of internal vowel change (<a href="/wiki/Apophony" title="Apophony">apophony</a>) and/or insertion (<a href="/wiki/Epenthesis" title="Epenthesis">epenthesis</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019266–267_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019266–267-160">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> These so-called <a href="/wiki/Broken_plural" title="Broken plural">"internal a" or "broken" plurals</a> are securely attested in Semitic, Berber, Cushitic, and Chadic, although it is unclear if the Chadic examples are an independent development.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGragg201940–41_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGragg201940–41-161">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;e&#93;</a></sup> Another common method of forming plurals is reduplication.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012538_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012538-164">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Noun_cases_and_states">Noun cases and states</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afroasiatic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Noun cases and states"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>

<p><a href="/wiki/Grammatical_case" title="Grammatical case">Nouns cases</a> are found in the Semitic, Berber, Cushitic, and Omotic. They are not found in Chadic languages, and there is no evidence for cases in Egyptian.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012534_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012534-165">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> A common pattern in AA languages with case is for the nominative to be marked by <i>-u</i> or <i>-i</i>, and the accusative to be marked by <i>-a</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019319_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019319-166">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> However, the number and types of cases varies across AA and also within the individual branches.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012534_165-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012534-165">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> Some languages in AA have a <a href="/wiki/Marked_nominative_alignment" title="Marked nominative alignment">marked nominative alignment</a>, a feature which may date back to Proto-Afroasiatic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012535–536_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012535–536-167">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGüldemann2018317_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGüldemann2018317-168">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> Zygmont Frajzyngier states that a general characteristic of case marking in AA languages is that it tends to mark roles such as genitive, dative, locative, etc. rather than the subject and object.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012535_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012535-169">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="font-size:smaller;">

</caption>

<p>A second category, which partially overlaps with case, is the AA linguistic category of "state." Linguists use the term "state" to refer to different things in different languages. In Cushitic and Semitic, nouns exist in the "free state" or the "construct state". The construct state is a special, usually reduced form of a noun, which is used when the noun is possessed by another noun (Semitic) or is modified by an adjective or relative clause (Cushitic). <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012533_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012533-170">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> Edward Lipiński refers to Semitic nouns as having four states: absolute (free/indeterminate), construct, determinate, and predicate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELipiński2001265_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELipiński2001265-171">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> Coptic and Egyptian grammar also refers to nouns having a "free" (absolute) state, a "construct state," and a "pronominal state." The construct state is used when a noun becomes unstressed as the first element of a compound, whereas the pronominal state is used when the noun has a suffixed possessive pronoun.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen202013_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen202013-172">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeylage201859_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeylage201859-173">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> Berber instead contrasts between the "free state" and the "annexed state," the latter of which is used for a variety of purposes, including for subjects placed after a verb and after certain prepositions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012533–534_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012533–534-174">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modifiers_and_agreement">Modifiers and agreement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afroasiatic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Modifiers and agreement"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There is no strict distinction between adjectives, nouns, and adverbs in Afroasaiatic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019312_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019312-175">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> All branches of Afroasiatic have a lexical category of <a href="/wiki/Adjective" title="Adjective">adjectives</a> except for Chadic;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012538_164-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012538-164">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> some Chadic languages do have adjectives, however. In Berber languages, adjectives are rare and are mostly replaced by nouns of quality and stative verbs. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019279_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019279-176">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> In different languages, adjectives (and other modifiers) must either precede or follow the noun.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019298_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019298-177">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> In most AA languages, numerals precede the noun.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019279_176-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019279-176">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In those languages that have adjectives, they can take gender and number markings, which, in some cases, agree with the gender and number of the noun they are modifying.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012538_164-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012538-164">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> However, in Omotic, adjectives do not  agree with nouns: sometimes, they only but take gender and number marking only when they are used as nouns, in other cases, they take gender and number marking only when they follow the noun (the noun then receives no marking).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012539–540_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012539–540-178">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<p>A widespread pattern of gender and number marking in Afroasiatic, found on demonstratives, articles, adjectives, and relative markers, is a consonant N for masculine, T for feminine, and N for plural. This can be found in Semitic, Egyptian, Beja, Berber, and Chadic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGüldemann2018316_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGüldemann2018316-181">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESouag2023308_158-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESouag2023308-158">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> A system K (masculine), T (feminine), and H (plural) can be found in Cushitic, Chadic, with masculine K also appearing in Omotic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngierShay201210_122-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngierShay201210-122">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHetzron2009548_119-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHetzron2009548-119">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> The feminine marker T is one of the most consistent aspects across the different branches of AA.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019266_157-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019266-157">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Verb_forms">Verb forms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afroasiatic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Verb forms"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span id="Tenses.2C_aspects.2C_and_moods_.28TAMs.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Tenses,_aspects,_and_moods_(TAMs)">Tenses, aspects, and moods (TAMs)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afroasiatic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Tenses, aspects, and moods (TAMs)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>There is no agreement about which <a href="/wiki/Tense-aspect-mood" class="mw-redirect" title="Tense-aspect-mood">tenses, aspects, or moods</a> (TAMs) Proto-Afroasiatic might have had.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019267_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019267-182">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> Most grammars of AA posit a distinction between <a href="/wiki/Perfective" class="mw-redirect" title="Perfective">perfective</a> and <a href="/wiki/Imperfective" class="mw-redirect" title="Imperfective">imperfective</a> verbal aspects, which can be found in Cushitic, Berber, Semitic, most Chadic languages, and some Omotic languages.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012592_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012592-183">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> The Egyptian verbal system diverges greatly from that found in the other branches.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019268_154-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019268-154">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, it is common in Afroasiatic languages for the present/imperfective form to be a derived (marked) form of the verb, whereas in most other languages and language families the present tense is the default form of the verb.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKouwenberg201090_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKouwenberg201090-184">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> Another common trait across the family is the use of a <a href="/wiki/Suppletion" title="Suppletion">suppletive</a> imperative for verbs of motion.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngierShay2020_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngierShay2020-185">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id=".22Prefix_conjugation.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="&quot;Prefix_conjugation&quot;">"Prefix conjugation"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afroasiatic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: &quot;Prefix conjugation&quot;"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Conjugation of verbs using prefixes that mark person, number, and gender can be found
in Semitic, Berber, and in Cushitic,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019268_154-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019268-154">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGragg201934_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGragg201934-186">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> where it is only found on a small set of frequent verbs.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMous2012391_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMous2012391-187">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> These prefixes are clearly cognate across the branches, although their use within the verbal systems of the individual languages varies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGragg201934_186-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGragg201934-186">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> There is a general pattern in which n- is used for the first person plural, whereas t- is used for all forms of the second person regardless of plurality or gender, as well as feminine singular.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGüldemann2018315_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGüldemann2018315-188">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> Prefixes of ʔ- (<a href="/wiki/Glottal_stop" title="Glottal stop">glottal stop</a>) for the first person singular and y- for the third person masculine can also be reconstructed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuehnergard2004140-141_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuehnergard2004140-141-189">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> As there is no evidence for the "prefix conjugation" in Omotic, Chadic, or Egyptian, it is unclear whether this was a Proto-Afroasiatic feature that has been lost in those branches or is a shared innovation among Semitic, Berber, and Cushitic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGragg201936_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGragg201936-144">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGüldemann2018315_188-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGüldemann2018315-188">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;h&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
