<h4><span id=".22Suffix_conjugation.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="&quot;Suffix_conjugation&quot;">"Suffix conjugation"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afroasiatic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: &quot;Suffix conjugation&quot;"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some AA branches have what is called a "suffix conjugation", formed by adding pronominal suffixes to indicate person, gender, and number to a verbal adjective.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019268_154-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019268-154">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuehnergard2004141_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuehnergard2004141-192">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> In Akkadian, Egyptian, Berber, and Cushitic this forms a "stative conjugation", used to express the state or result of an action; the same endings as in Akkadian and Egyptian are also present in the West Semitic perfective verb form.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGragg201933_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGragg201933-193">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELipiński2001360_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELipiński2001360-194">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> In Akkadian and Egyptian, the suffixes appear to be reduced forms of the independent pronouns (see <a href="#Pronouns">Pronouns</a>); the obvious correspondence between the endings in the two branches has been argued to show that Egyptian and Semitic are closely related.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGragg201933-24_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGragg201933-24-195">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> While some scholars posit an AA origin for this form, it is possible that the Berber and Cushitic forms are independent developments,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019268_154-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019268-154">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> as they show significant differences from the Egyptian and Semitic forms. The Cushitic forms in particular may be derived from morphology found in subordinate clauses.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGragg201934_186-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGragg201934-186">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Common_derivational_affixes">Common derivational affixes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afroasiatic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Common derivational affixes"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="M-prefix_noun_derivation">M-prefix noun derivation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afroasiatic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: M-prefix noun derivation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A prefix in m- is the most widely attested affix in AA that is used to derive nouns,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019266_157-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019266-157">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilson2020123_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilson2020123-196">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> and is one the features <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Greenberg" title="Joseph Greenberg">Joseph Greenberg</a> used to diagnose membership in the family. It forms <a href="/wiki/Agent_noun" title="Agent noun">agent nouns</a>, place nouns, and instrument nouns.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngierShay201210_122-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngierShay201210-122">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuehnergard2004140_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuehnergard2004140-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> In some branches, it can also derive abstract nouns and participles.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilson2020123_196-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilson2020123-196">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> Omotic, meanwhile, shows evidence for a non-productive prefix mV- associated with the feminine gender.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShay2014577_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShay2014577-197">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> Christopher Ehret has argued that this prefix is a later development that was not present in Proto-Afro-Asiatic, but rather derived from a PAA indefinite pronoun *m-.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEhret199552_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEhret199552-198">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> Such an etymology is rejected by A. Zaborski and Gábor Takács, the latter of whom argues for a PAA <i>*ma-</i> that unites all or some of the meanings in the modern languages.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETakács20088_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETakács20088-199">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Verbal_extensions">Verbal extensions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afroasiatic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Verbal extensions"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<table class="wikitable" style="font-size:smaller;">
<caption>Common verbal extensions in Afroasiatic, using data from <a href="#CITEREFWilson2020">Wilson 2020</a>, <a href="#CITEREFBubenik2023">Bubenik 2023</a>, and <a href="#CITEREFKossmann2007">Kossmann 2007</a>
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th>Language</th>
<th>Causative *s-</th>
<th>Reflexive/middle *t-</th>
<th>Passive *n-
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>Akkadian (Semitic)</td>
<td>u-š-apris 'make cut'</td>
<td>mi-t-gurum 'agree (with one another)'</td>
<td>i-p-paris (&gt; *i-n-paris) 'be cut'
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Figuig (Berber)</td>
<td>ssu-fəɣ 'let out'</td>
<td>i-ttə-ska 'it has been built'</td>
<td>mmu-bḍa 'divide oneself'
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Beja (Cushitic)</td>
<td>s-dabil 'make gather'</td>
<td>t-dabil 'be gathered'</td>
<td>m-dabaal 'gather each other'
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Egyptian</td>
<td>s-ꜥnḫ 'make live'</td>
<td>pr-tj 'is sent forth'<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;i&#93;</a></sup></td>
<td>n-hp 'escape'<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;j&#93;</a></sup>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Many AA languages use prefixes or suffixes (verbal extensions) to encode various pieces of information about the verb.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012525_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012525-204">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> Three <a href="/wiki/Derivation_(linguistics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Derivation (linguistics)">derivational</a> prefixes can be reconstructed for Proto-Afroasiatic: *s- '<a href="/wiki/Causative" title="Causative">causative</a>', *t- '<a href="/wiki/Middle_voice" class="mw-redirect" title="Middle voice">middle voice</a>' or '<a href="/wiki/Reflexive_verb" title="Reflexive verb">reflexive</a>', and *n- '<a href="/wiki/Passive_Voice" class="mw-redirect" title="Passive Voice">passive</a>';<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuehnergard2004141_192-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuehnergard2004141-192">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> the prefixes appear with various related meanings in the individual daughter languages and branches.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStauder202387_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStauder202387-205">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> Christopher Ehret has proposed that Proto-Afroasiatic originally had as many as thirty-seven separate verbal extensions, many of which then became fossilized as third consonants.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEhret199527–34_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEhret199527–34-206">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> This theory has been criticized by some, such as Andrzej Zaborski and Alan Kaye, as being too many extensions to be realistic, though Zygmont Frajzyngier and Erin Shay note that some Chadic languages have as many as twelve extensions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngierShay201213_96-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngierShay201213-96">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id=".22Nisba.22_derivation"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="&quot;Nisba&quot;_derivation"><span class="anchor" id="nisba"></span>"Nisba" derivation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afroasiatic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: &quot;Nisba&quot; derivation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The so-called "<a href="/wiki/Arabic_nouns_and_adjectives#Nisba" title="Arabic nouns and adjectives">Nisba</a>" is a suffix used to derive adjectives from nouns and, in Egyptian, also from prepositions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeylage2018115_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeylage2018115-207">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> It is found in Egyptian, Semitic, and possibly, in some relic forms, Berber.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilson202047_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilson202047-208">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> The suffix has the same basic form in Egyptian and Semitic,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeylage2018115_207-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeylage2018115-207">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> taking the form -i(y) in Semitic and being written -j in Egyptian. The Semitic and Cushitic genitive case in -i/-ii may be related to "nisba" adjective derivation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuehnergard2004148_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuehnergard2004148-209">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeust2012243_162-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeust2012243-162">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="font-size:smaller;">
<caption>"Nisba" derivation in Semitic and Egyptian, using data from <a href="#CITEREFWilson2020">Wilson 2020</a> and <a href="#CITEREFBeylage2018">Beylage 2018</a>
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th>Language</th>
<th>Noun/preposition</th>
<th>Derived adjective
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>Hebrew (Semitic)</td>
<td>yārēaḥ <i>moon</i></td>
<td>yərēḥī <i> lunar</i>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td rowspan="2">Egyptian</td>
<td>nṯr <i>god</i></td>
<td>nṯr.j <i>divine</i>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>ḥr <i>upon</i></td>
<td>ḥr.j <i>upper, which is upon</i>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Due to its presence in the oldest attested and best-known AA branches, nisba derivation is often thought of as a "quintessentially Afroasiatic feature".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeust2012243_162-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeust2012243-162">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilson2020168_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilson2020168-210">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> Christopher Ehret argues for its presence in Proto-Afroasiatic and for its attestation in some form in all branches, with a shape <i>-*ay</i> in addition to -<i>*iy</i> in some cases.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEhret199516_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEhret199516-211">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocabulary_comparison">Vocabulary comparison</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afroasiatic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Vocabulary comparison"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pronouns">Pronouns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afroasiatic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Pronouns"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The forms of the pronouns are very stable throughout Afroasiatic (excluding Omotic),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019269_151-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019269-151">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> and they have been used as one of the chief tools for determining whether a language belongs to the family.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGüldemann2018315-316_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGüldemann2018315-316-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> However, there is no consensus on what the reconstructed set of Afroasiatic pronouns might have looked like.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGragg201929_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGragg201929-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> A common characteristic of AA languages is the existence of a special set of "independent" pronouns, which are distinct from subject pronouns. They can occur together with subject pronouns but cannot fulfill an object function.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012523–524_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngier2012523–524-212">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> Also common are dependent/affix pronouns (used for direct objects and to mark possession).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGragg201929_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGragg201929-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> For most branches, the first person pronouns contain a <a href="/wiki/Nasal_consonant" title="Nasal consonant">nasal consonant</a> (n, m), whereas the third person displays a <a href="/wiki/Sibilant_consonant" class="mw-redirect" title="Sibilant consonant">sibilant consonant</a> (s, sh).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGüldemann2018314-315_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGüldemann2018314-315-213">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> Other commonalities are masculine and feminine forms used in both the second and third persons, except in Cushitic and Omotic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019269_151-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019269-151">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> These pronouns tend to show a masculine "u" and a feminine "i".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHetzron2009548_119-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHetzron2009548-119">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> The Omotic forms of the personal pronouns differ from the others, with only the plural forms in North Omotic appearing potentially to be cognate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGragg201932_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGragg201932-214">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Numerals">Numerals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afroasiatic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Numerals"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Unlike in the <a href="/wiki/Indo-European" class="mw-redirect" title="Indo-European">Indo-European</a> or <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_languages" title="Austronesian languages">Austronesian</a> language families, numerals in AA languages cannot be traced to a proto-system.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZaborski1987317_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZaborski1987317-215">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKayeDaniels1992439_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKayeDaniels1992439-216">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> The Cushitic and Chadic numeral systems appear to have originally been base 5. The system in Berber, Egyptian, and Semitic, however, has independent words for the numbers 6–9.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZaborski1987317–318_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZaborski1987317–318-217">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> Thus, it is possible that the numerals in Egyptian, Berber, and Semitic are more closely related, whereas the Cushitic and Chadic numerals are more closely related to each other.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELipiński2001280_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELipiński2001280-218">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKayeDaniels1992440–441_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKayeDaniels1992440–441-219">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> Modern Chadic numeral systems are sometimes <a href="/wiki/Decimal" title="Decimal">decimal</a>, having separate names for the numbers 1–10, and sometimes <a href="/wiki/Quinary" title="Quinary">base-5</a>, deriving the numbers 6–9 from the numbers 1–5 in some way.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngierShay2012a273_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrajzyngierShay2012a273-220">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> Some families show more than one word for a numeral: Chadic, Semitic, and Berber each have two words for two,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKayeDaniels1992440_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKayeDaniels1992440-221">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELipiński2001284_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELipiński2001284-222">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> and Semitic has four words for one.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELipiński2001281_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELipiński2001281-223">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> Andrzej Zaborski further notes that the numbers "one", "two", and "five" are particularly susceptible to replacement by new words, with "five" often based on a word meaning "hand".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZaborski1987317_215-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZaborski1987317-215">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Another factor making comparisons of AA numeral systems difficult is the possibility of <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">borrowing</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKayeDaniels1992439_216-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKayeDaniels1992439-216">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> Only some Berber languages maintain the native Berber numeral system, with many using Arabic loans for higher numbers and some from any numeral beyond two.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019295_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019295-224">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> In some Berber languages, the roots for one and two are also borrowed from Arabic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELipiński2001280-281_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELipiński2001280-281-225">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> Some South Cushitic numerals are borrowed from <a href="/wiki/Nilotic_languages" title="Nilotic languages">Nilotic languages</a>, other Cushitic numerals have been borrowed from <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Semitic" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethiopian Semitic">Ethiopian Semitic</a> languages.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZaborski1987325_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZaborski1987325-226">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="font-size:smaller;">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cognates">Cognates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afroasiatic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Cognates"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Afroasiatic languages share a vocabulary of Proto-Afroasiatic origin to varying extents.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019248_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerWolff2019248-227">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> Writing in 2004, John Huehnergard notes the great difficulty in establishing cognate sets across the family.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuehnergard2004141_192-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuehnergard2004141-192">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> Identifying cognates is difficult because the languages in question are often separated by thousands of years of development and many languages within the family have long been in contact with each other, raising the possibility of loanwords.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGragg201941_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGragg201941-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Work is also hampered because of the poor state of documentation of many languages.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPorkhomovsky2020273_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPorkhomovsky2020273-228">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There are two etymological dictionaries of Afroasiatic, one by Christopher Ehret, and one by Vladimir Orel and Olga Stolbova, both from 1995. Both works provide highly divergent reconstructions and have been heavily criticized by other scholars.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGüldemann2018317-318_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGüldemann2018317-318-229">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> Andrzej Zaborski refers to Orel and Stolbova's reconstructions as "controversial", and Ehret's as "not acceptable to many scholars".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZaborski2011_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZaborski2011-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Tom Güldemann argues that much comparative work in Afroasiatic suffers from not attempting first to reconstruct smaller units within the individual branches, but instead comparing words in the individual languages.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGüldemann2018318_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGüldemann2018318-230">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, both dictionaries agree on some items and some proposed cognates are uncontroversial.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGragg201941_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGragg201941-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuehnergard2004141_192-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuehnergard2004141-192">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> Such cognates tend to rely on relatively simple <a href="/wiki/Sound_correspondence" class="mw-redirect" title="Sound correspondence">sound correspondences</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHayward200094_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHayward200094-115">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="font-size:smaller;">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Afroasiatic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Notes"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-63">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In this display, the universally recognized primary branches of AA are in <b>bold</b>. Individual languages designated as primary branches are in <i>italics</i>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-64"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-64">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Old East Africa Cushitic =South Cushitic and <a href="/wiki/Yaaku_language" title="Yaaku language">Yaaku</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-65"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-65">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Fleming excludes South Cushitic, Yaaku, and Beja from Cushitic proper.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-121"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-121">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Some scholars reconstruct "emphatic" consonants for Egyptian and some do not.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuehnergard2023142_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuehnergard2023142-120">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-163"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-163">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As Egyptian is spelled without vowels, it is difficult to know whether it had internal change plurals. There is some evidence from Coptic, but this may be unrelated to AA. There is also some evidence from <a href="/wiki/Cuneiform" title="Cuneiform">cuneiform</a> transcriptions of Egyptian words.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeust2012243_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeust2012243-162">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-179"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-179">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Forms that do not follow the pattern are in parentheses.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-180"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-180">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Some languages have an additional feminine plural form in -t that is not listed here.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-191"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-191">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Traditionally, the Hausa subject pronouns have been compared to the prefix conjugation. However, since the 1970s and '80s, comparisons of other Chadic subject pronouns with the Hausa ones have convinced most scholars that the similarity to the prefix conjugation is incidental.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeust2012238–239_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeust2012238–239-190">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-201"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-201">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Egyptian passive suffix is solely morphological and does not form a unique stem.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStauder202388–90_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStauder202388–90-200">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-203"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-203">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Egyptian prefix has a middle voice/intransitive/or passive meaning.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen201394_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen201394-202">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-231"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-231">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Many of these roots have other proposed cognates that are not included on the table.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-232"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-232">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A <a href="/wiki/Caron" title="Caron">caron</a> ˇ over a vowel indicates rising <a href="/wiki/Tone_(linguistics)" title="Tone (linguistics)">tone</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Circumflex" title="Circumflex">circumflex</a> ^ over a vowel indicates falling tone. V indicates a <a href="/wiki/Vowel" title="Vowel">vowel</a> of unknown quality. ʔ indicates a <a href="/wiki/Glottal_stop" title="Glottal stop">glottal stop</a>. * indicates <a href="/wiki/Linguistic_reconstruction" title="Linguistic reconstruction">reconstructed forms</a> based on <a href="/wiki/Comparative_method_(linguistics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Comparative method (linguistics)">comparison of related languages</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-233"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-233">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> Ehret's dictionary lists "Proto-South-Cushitic <i>*daaʕ-</i> 'to rend, tear', a root he reconstructs from the two words listed here in <a href="#CITEREFEhret1980">Ehret 1980</a>.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
